Item $\ref{corollary-3-2}$ and Corollary $\ref{corollary-2}$ show that

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathcal{P}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) =& 3K + 4 = 3(K + 1) + 1 \\
  =& 3(d_1 (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) + 1 \leq \frac{3L(b - a)}{\epsilon} + 7
\end{equation*}
$$

---
**Item** $\ref{corollary-3-7}$

From Lemma $\ref{lemma-4}$, each summand in $\eqref{equation-43}$ has the structure

$$
  c_k \circledast (\mathfrak{i}_1 \circ \mathbf{A}_{1, -r_k) = ((1, -r_k), (c_k, 0))
$$

such that

$$
  \boldsymbol{\Lambda} = \left(\mathbf{1}, \left[\begin{smallmatrix} -r_0 \\ \vdots \\ -r_K \end{smallmatrix}\right], ((c_0,\dots, c_K), f(r_0)) \right) \in (\R^{(K+1)\times 1} \times \R^{K+1}) \times (\R^{1\times (K+1)} \times\R)
$$

Hence, the operator norm of $\mathcal{T}$ is

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \norm{\mathcal{T}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda})}_\infty = \max\set{|r_0|, \dots, |r_K|, |c_0|, \dots, |c_K|, |f(r_0)|, 1}
\tag{\label{equation-42}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since for all $k \in\N_+ \cap (0, K+1)$

$$
  r_k - r_{k-1} = \frac{b - a}{\max\set{K,1}}
$$

the Lipschitz continuity of $f$, implies for all $k=0,\dots K$

$$
\begin{align*}
  c_k \leq& \frac{K(|f(r_{\min\set{k+1,K}}) - f(r_k)| + |f(m_{\max\set{k-1, 0}}) - f(r_k)|}{b - a} \\
  \leq& \frac{KL (|r_{\min\set{k+1,K}} - r_k| + |r_{\max\set{k-1, 0}} - r_k|)}{b - a} \\
  \leq& \frac{2KL(b-a) [\max\set{K,1}]^{-1}}{b - a} \leq 2L
\end{align*}
$$

Combining with $\eqref{equation-42}$ proves item $\ref{corollary-3-7}$
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="Implicit one-dimensional neural network approximations (strong form)" boxType="corollary" tag="4">
For $a,b\in\R$ with $a < b$ let $f:[a,b]\to\R$ be a Lipschitz continuous function with Lipschitz constant $L \in(0,\infty)$. Let $r:\R\to[0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. Then there exists $M \in\R$ such that for all $\epsilon \in (0, 1]$ there exists a fully-connected feedforward neural network $\boldsymbol{\Lambda}\in\mathcal{N}$ with the following properties.

1. **Hidden layers:** $\mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) = 1$
2. **Total number of parameters:** $\mathcal{P}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) \leq M\epsilon^{-1}$
3. $\norm{\mathcal{T}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda})}_\infty \leq \max\set{1, |a|, |b|, 2L, |f(a)}$
4. **Approximation error:** The realization $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) \in C(\R)$ satisfies
$$
  \sup_{x\in[a,b]} |(\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}))(x) - f(x)| \leq \epsilon
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Let $M = 3L(b - a) + 7$, then

$$
\begin{align*}
  3L(b - a)\epsilon^{-1} + 7 \leq 3L(b - a)\epsilon^{-1} + 7\epsilon^{-1} \\
  =& M\epsilon^{-1}
\end{align*}
$$

This and Corollary $\ref{corollary-3}$ prove the items.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="Implicit one-dimensional neural network approximations (weak form)" boxType="corollary" tag="5">
For $a,b\in\R$ with $a < b$ let $f:[a,b]\to\R$ be a Lipschitz continuous function with Lipschitz constant $L \in(0,\infty)$. Let $r:\R\to[0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. Then there exists $M \in\R$ such that for all $\epsilon \in (0, 1]$ there exists a fully-connected feedforward neural network $\boldsymbol{\Lambda}\in\mathcal{N}$ with the following properties.

1. **Total number of parameters:** $\mathcal{P}(\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) \leq M\epsilon^{-1}$
2. **Approximation error:** The realization $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}) \in C(\R)$ satisfies
$$
  \sup_{x\in[a,b]} |(\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}))(x) - f(x)| \leq \epsilon
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

This follows from Corollary $\ref{corollary-4}$.
</details>
</MathBox>

## Multi-dimensional approximation

### Approximations through supremal convolutions

<MathBox title="" boxType="proposition" tag="19">
Let 
- $(E, d)$ be a metric space
- $D\subseteq E$ be a subset
- $M \subseteq D$ be nonempty, $M \neq\emptyset$

Suppose $f:D\to\R$ is a Lipschitz continuous function with Lipschitz constant $L \in (0,\infty)$, i.e. for all $x\in D$ and $y\in M$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  |f(x) - f(y)| \leq Ld(x,y)
\tag{\label{equation-44}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Define $F:E\to\R \cap \set{\infty}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  F(x) := \sup_{y\in M} [f(y) - Ld(x,y)]
\tag{\label{equation-45}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then $F$ is well-defined has the following properties.

1. **Agreement on $M$:** $F(x) = f(x)$ for all $x\in M$
2. **Domination on $D$:** $F(x) \leq f(x)$ for all $x\in D$
3. **Finiteness on $E$:** $F(x) < \infty$ for all $x\in E$
4. **Global Lipschitz continuity:** $|F(x) - F(y)| \leq L\delta(x,y)$ for all $x,y \in E$
5. **Deviation from $f$ on $D$:** for all $x\in D$
$$
  |F(x) - f(x)| \leq 2L \inf_{y\in M} [d(x,y)]
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

**Item** $\ref{proposition-19-2}$

From $\eqef{equation-44}$, it follows that for all $x\in D$ and $y\in M$

$$
  f(y) + Ld(x,y) \geq f(x) \geq f(y) - Ld(x,y)
$$

implying for all $x\in D$

$$
  f(x) \geq \sup_{y\in M} [f(y) - Ld(x,y)] = F(x)
$$

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-19-1}$

From $\eqref{equation-45}$, it follows that for all $x\in M$

$$
  F(x) \geq f(x) - Ld(x,x) = f(x)
$$

Together with $\eqref{equation-46}$, this shows that

$$
  F(x) \leq f(x)
$$

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-19-3}$

Applying the triangle inequality to $\eqref{equation-46}$, we obtain for all $x\in E$ and $y, z\in M$

$$
\begin{align*}
  f(y) - Ld(x, y) \tleq& f(z) + Ld(y, z) - Ld(x, y) \\
  \leq& f(z) + Ld(x, z)
\end{align*}
$$

Hence, for all $x\in E$ and $z\in M$

$$
\begin{align*}
  F(x) =& \sup_{y\in M} [f(y) - Ld(x,y)] \\
  \leq& f(z) + Ld(x,z) < \infty
\end{align*}
$$

This and the assumption $M \neq\emptyset$ proves the item.

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-19-4}$

From $\eqref{equation-45}$ and item $\ref{proposition-19-3}$, we have for all $x,y \in E$

$$
\begin{align*}
  F(x) - F(y) =& \sup_{v\in M} [f(v) - Ld(x,v)] - \sup_{w\in M} [f(w) - Ld(y,w)] \\
  =& \sup_{v\in M} \left[f(v) - Ld(x,y) - \sup_{w\in M} [f(w) - Ld(y,w)] \right] \\
  \leq& \sup_{v\in M} \left[f(v) - Ld(x,v) - (f(v) - Ld(y,v)) \right] \\
  =& \sup_{v\in M} [Ld(y,v) - Ld(x,v)] \\
  \leq& \sup_{v\in M} [Ld(y,x) + Ld(x,v) - Ld(x,v)] \\
  =& Ld(x,y)
\end{align*}
$$

By reflexivity of metrics, $d(x,y) = d(y,x)$, proving the item. 

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-19-5}$

$$
\begin{align*}
  |F(x) - f(x)| =& \inf_{y\in M} |F(x) - F(y) + f(y) - f(x)| \\
  \leq& \inf_{y\in M} (|F(x) - F(y)| + |f(y) - f(x)|) \\
  \leq& \int_{y\in M} (2L\delta(x,y)) = 2L \inf_{y\in M} [d(x,y)]
\end{align*}
$$

</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="" boxType="corollary" tag="7">
Let 
- $(E, d)$ be a metric space
- $M \subseteq D$ be nonempty, $M \neq\emptyset$

Suppose $f:E\to\R$ is a Lipschitz continuous function with Lipschitz constant $L \in (0,\infty)$, i.e. for all $x\in E$ and $y\in M$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  |f(x) - f(y)| \leq Ld(x,y)
\tag{\label{equation-44}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Define $F:E\to\R \cap \set{\infty}$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  F(x) := \sup_{y\in M} [f(y) - Ld(x,y)]
\tag{\label{equation-45}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Then $F$ is well-defined has the following properties.

1. **Agreement on $M$:** $F(x) = f(x)$ for all $x\in M$
2. **Domination on $E$:** $F(x) \leq f(x)$ for all $x\in E$
4. **Global Lipschitz continuity:** $|F(x) - F(y)| \leq L\delta(x,y)$ for all $x,y \in E$
5. **Deviation from $f$ on $E$:** for all $x\in E$
$$
  |F(x) - f(x)| \leq 2L \inf_{y\in M} [d(x,y)]
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

This follows from Proposition $\ref{proposition-19}$.
</details>
</MathBox>

### Neural network representations

#### $\ell_1$-norm

<MathBox title="$\ell_1$-norm neural network" boxType="proposition" tag="20">
Define the fully-connected feedforward neural networks $(\mathbf{L}_d)_{d\in\N_+} \subseteq\mathcal{N}$ recursively by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathbf{L}_1 = \left(\left(\left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \end{smallmatrix}\right], \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \end{smallmatrix}\right] \right), \left(\left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right], \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 0\end{smallmatrix}\right] \right)\right) \in (\R^{2\times \R^2) \times\R^2) \times (\R^{1\times 2} \times\R^1)
\tag{\label{equation-47}}
\end{equation*}
$$

and for $d=1,\dots$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathbf{L}_d = \mathbf{S}_{1,d} \circ \mathbf{P}_d (\underbrace{\mathbf{L}_1,\dots,\mathbf{L}_1}_{d \text{ times}})
\tag{\label{equation-48}}
\end{equation*}
$$

The networks $\mathbf{L}_d$ has the following properties.

1. **Dimension vector:** $\mathbf{d}(\mathbf{L}_d) = (d,2d,1)$
2. **Realization under ReLU:** Let $r: \R\to[0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. For $d\in\N_+$ the realization $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{L}_d) \in C(\R^d, \R)$ is given by
$$
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{L}_d))(\mathbf{x}) = \norm{\mathbf{x}}_1
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

**Item** $\ref{proposition-20-1}$

Since $\mathbf{d}(\mathbf{L}_1) = (1,2,1)$ it follows from Definition $\ref{definition-1}$ that

$$
  \mathbf{d}(\mathbf{P}_d (\mathbf{L}_1,\dots,\mathbf{L}_1) = (d,2d,d)
$$

Combining this with Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$ and $\ref{proposition-8}$, we get

$$
  \mathbf{d}(\boldsymbol{L}_d) = \mathbf{d}(\mathbf{S}_{1,d} \circ \mathbf{P}_d (\mathbf{L}_1,\dots,\mathbf{L}_1)) = (d,2d,1)
$$

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-20-2}$

From $\eqref{equation-47}$, we get

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{L}_d))(\mathbf{x}) =& r(\mathbf{x}) + r(-\mathbf{x}) \\
  =& \max\set{x, 0} + \max\set{-x, 0} \\
  =& |x| = \norm{x}_1
\end{align*}
$$

By Proposition $\ref{proposition-10}$ we get

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{L}_d))(\mathbf{x}) =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{S}_{1,d} \circ \mathbf{P}_d (\mathbf{L}_1,\dots,\mathbf{L}_1)))(\mathbf{x}) \\
  =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{S}_{1,d}))(|x_1|,\dots,|x_d|) \\
  =& \sum_{k=1}^d |x_k| = \norm{x}_1
\end{align*}
$$

</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma" tag="5">
For $d\in\N_+$, let $\mathbf{L}_d \in\mathcal{N}$ be $\ell_1$-norm neural networks defined in Proposition $\ref{proposition-20}$. Then

1. $\mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} = \mathbf{0} \in\R^{2d}$
2. $\mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{L}_d} = 0 \in\R$
3. $\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \in\set{-1, 0, 1}^{(2d)\times d}$
4. $\norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathbf{x}}_\infty = \norm{\mathbf{x}}$ for all $\mathbf{x} \in\R^d$
5. $\mathcal{W}_{2,\mathbf{L}_d} = \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & \cdots & 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right]$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Assume without loss of generality that $d > 1$; the case $d = 1$ follows directly from the base construction in Proposition $\ref{proposition-20}$

---
**Items $\ref{lemma-5-1}$ and $\ref{lemma-5-2}$**

For $d = 1$, the bias vectors of the network $\mathbf{L}_1$ are given by
- $\mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{L}_1} = \mathbf{0} \in\R^2$
- $\mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{L}_1} = \mathbf{0} \in\R$

By $\eqref{equation-48}$, the network $\mathbf{L}_d$ is obtained by stacking $d$ copies of $\mathbf{L}_1$ without inducing additional bias terms. Hence all hidden layer and output layer biases remain zero.

---
**Item** $\ref{lemma-5-3}$

For $d=1$ the hidden-layer weight matrix of $\mathbf{L}_1$ is given by $\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_1} = \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \end{smallmatrix}\right]$. By construction

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_1} = \operatorname{diag}(\mathcal{W}_{1,\mathbf{L}_1},\dots,\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_1}) \in \R^{(2d)\times d}
\tag{\label{equation-49}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Each block contributes only entries in $\set{-1,0,1}$, proving the item.

---
**Item** $\ref{lemma-5-4}$

Using $\eqref{equation-49}$, we have for $\mathbf{x} \in \R^d$

$$
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathbf{x} = (x_1, -x_1, \dots, x_d, -x_d)^\top
$$

and thus

$$
 \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathbf{x}}_\infty = \max_{i \in[d]} |x_i| = \norm{\mathbf{x}}_\infty
$$

---
**Item** $\ref{\lemma-5-5}$

For $d=1$, the output weight matrix of $\mathbb{L}_1$ is given by $\mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{L}_1} = \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right]$.

From $\eqref{equation-48}$ we have

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{L}_d} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{S}_{1,d}} \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{P}_d (\mathbf{L}_1,\dots,\mathbf{L}_1)} \\
  =& \underbrace{\left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & \cdots & 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right]}_{\in\R^{1\times d}} \underbrace{\operatorname{diag}(\mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{L}_1},\dots,\mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{L}_1})}_{\in\R^{d\times (2d)}} \\
  =& \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & \cdots & 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right] \in \R^{1\times (2d)}
\end{align*}
$$
</details>
</MathBox>

#### Maxima

<MathBox title="Unique existence of fully-connected feedforward maxima neural networks" boxType="lemma" tag="6">
There exists unique fully-connected neural networks $(\boldsymbol{\phi}_d)_{d\in\N} \subset\mathcal{N}$ that satisfy the following properties

1. **Input dimension:** $\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\phi}_d) = d$
2. **Output dimension:** $\mathcal{O}(\boldsymbol{\phi}_d) = 1$
3. **Base case $d=1$:** $\boldsymbol{\phi}_1 = \mathbf{A}_{1,0} \in\R^{1\times 1} \times \R$
4. **Base case $d=2$:** $\boldsymbol{\phi}_2 \in (\R^{3\times 2} \times\R^3) \times (\R^{1\times 3} \times \R)$ is given by
$$
\begin{equation*}
  \boldsymbol{\phi}_2 = \left(\left(\left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & - 1 \\ 0 & 1 \\ 0 & -1 \end{smallmatrix}\right], [\begin{smallmatrix} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{smallmatrix}\right]\right), \left([\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & 1 & - 1 \end{smallmatrix}\right], [\begin{smallmatrix} 0 \end{smallmatrix}\right] \right) \right)
\tag{\label{equation-50}}
\end{equation*}
$$

5. **Even recursion:** for all $d=2,\dots$
$$
  \boldsymbol{\phi}_{2d} = \boldsymbol{\phi}_d \circ \mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\phi}_2, \dots, \boldsymbol{\phi}_2)
$$

6. **Odd recursion:** for all $d=2,\dots$
$$
  \boldsymbol{\phi}_{2d-1} = \boldsymbol{\phi}_d \circ \mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\phi}_2, \dots, \boldsymbol{\phi}_2, \mathfrak{I}_1)
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Define $\boldsymbol{\psi} := \boldsymbol{\phi}_2 \in(\R^{3\times 2} \times\R^3) \times (\R^{1\times 3} \times \R)$ by

Using Proposition $\ref{proposition-3}$, the network $\boldsymbol{\psi}$ has the structure
- $\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\psi}) = 2$
- $\mathcal{O}(\boldsymbol{\psi}) = \mathcal{O}(\mathfrak{I}_1) = 1$
- $\mathcal{L}(\boldsymbol{\psi}) = \mathcal{L}(\mathfrak{I}_1) = 2$

From Definition $\ref{definition-3}$ and Proposition $\ref{proposition-3}$, the $d$-parallelization of $\boldsymbol{\psi}$ has the structure
- $\mathcal{I}(\mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\psi},\dots,\boldsymbol{\psi})) = 2d$
- $\mathcal{O}(\mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\psi},\dots,\boldsymbol{\psi})) = d$
- $\mathcal{I}(\mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\psi},\dots,\boldsymbol{\psi}, \mathfrak{I}_1)) = 2d - 1$
- $\mathcal{O}(\mathbf{P}_d (\boldsymbol{\psi},\dots,\boldsymbol{\psi}, \mathfrak{I}_1)) = d$

By Proposition $\ref{proposition-1}$, network composition is well-defined whenever input-output dimensions match. Hence items $\ref{lemma-6-5}$ and $\ref{lemma-6-6}$ define a valid network $\boldsymbol{\phi}_d$ for every $d\geq 3$. Every integer $d\geq 1$ is obtained by uniquely iterating the maps $d \mapsto \lfloor d/2 \right\rfloor$ and $d \mapsto \lceil d/2 \right\rceil$. By construction, $\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\phi}_d) = d$ and $\mathcal{O}(\boldsymbol{\phi}_d) = 1$ for all $d\in\N_+$, and the base cases $\ref{lemma-6-3}$ and $\ref{lemma-6-4}$ hold by definition.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="Maxima neural network" boxType="proposition" tag="21">
Let $(\mathbf{M}_d)_{d\in\N} \subseteq \mathcal{N}$ denote the collection of fully-connected feedforward maxima neural networks constructed in Lemma $\ref{lemma-6}$. For each $d\in\N_+$, the network $\mathbf{M}_d$ satisfies the following properties.

1. **Number of hidden layers:** $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_d) = \lceil \log_2 (d) \rceil$
2. **Dimension function:** for all $i\in\N$, then 
$$
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_d) \leq 3 \left\lceil \frac{d}{2^i} \right\rceil
$$

3. **Realization under ReLU:** Let $r:\R\to [0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. The realization $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{M}_d) \in C(\R^d, \R)$ is given by
$$
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{M}_d))(\mathbf{x}) = \max\set{x_1,\dots,x_d}
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

**Item** $\ref{proposition-21-1}$

By construction in $\eqref{equation-50}$ the base network $\mathbf{M}_2$ has two affine layers, i.e. $\mathcal{L}(\boldsymbol{M}_2) = 2$, hence

$$
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_2) = \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{M}_2) - 1 = 1
$$

By Definition $\ref{definition-3-1}$, parallelization does not increase depth, and therefore for all $\mathfrak{d} \geq 2$

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{P}_\mathfrak{d} (\mathbf{M}_2,\dots,\mathbf{M}_2)) =& \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{P}_\mathfrak{d} (\mathbf{M}_2,\dots,\mathbf{M}_2, \mathfrak{I}_1)) \\
  =& \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_2) = 1
\end{align*}
$$

By Proposition $\ref{proposition-1-3}$, composition adds hidden layers so that for all $\mathfrak{d} \geq 3$

$$
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) = \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_{\lceil\mathfrak{d}/2 \rceil}) + 1
$$

This assures that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 4,6,\dots$ with $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_{\mathfrak{d}/2}) = \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}/2) \rceil$

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}/2) \rceil + 1 \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}) - 1 \rceil + 1 \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}) \rceil
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-51}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $\lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d} + 1)\rceil = \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}) \rceil$, it follows that for all $\mathfrak{d}=3,5,\dots$ with $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_{\mathfrak{d}/2}) = \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}/2) \rceil$

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{H} (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}/2) \rceil + 1 \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 ((\mathfrak{d} + 1)/2) \rceil + 1 \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d} + 1) - 1 \rceil + 1 \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d} + 1) \rceil \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}) \rceil
\end{align*}
$$

Combining this and $\eqref{equation-51}$ shows that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 3,4,\dots$ with $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_k) = \lceil \log_2 (k) \rceil$ for all $k=2,\dots,\mathfrak{d} - 1$

$$
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) = \lceil \log_2 (\mathfrak{d}) \rceil
$$

The item follows by induction.

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-21-2}$

The base network $\mathbf{M}_2$ has dimension vector $\mathbf{d}(\mathbf{M}_2) = (2,3,1)$, hence for $i\in\N_+$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_2) \leq 3 = 3 \lceil \frac{2}{2^i} \rceil
\tag{\label{equation-56}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Proposition $\ref{proposition-1}$ and Definition $\ref{definition-3}$ imply for all $\mathfrak{d}=2,3,\dots$ and $i\in\N_+$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}) = \begin{cases}
    3\mathfrak{d},\quad& i = 1 \\
    d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}),\quad& i \geq 2
  \end{cases}
\tag{\label{equation-54}}
\end{equation*}
$$

and

$$
\begin{equation*}
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1) = \begin{cases}
    3\mathfrak{d} - 1,\quad& i = 1 \\
    d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}),\quad& i \geq 2
  \end{cases}
\tag{\label{equation-52}}
\end{equation*}
$$

This assures that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 2,4,\dots$

$$
\begin{equation*}
  d_1 (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) = d \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2} = 3\left\lceil\frac{\mathfrak{d}{2}} \right\rceil
\tag{\label{equation-53}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Additionally, $\eqref{equation-52}$ ensures that for all $d=3,5,\dots$

$$
  d_1 (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) = 3\left\lceil\frac{\mathfrak{d}{2}} \right\rceil - 1 \leq 3 \left\lceil\frac{\mathfrak{d}{2}} \right\rceil
$$

This and $\eqref{equation-53}$ show that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 2,3,\dots$

$$
  d_1 (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) \leq 3 \left\lceil\frac{\mathfrak{d}{2}} \right\rceil
$$

Note that $\eqref{equation-54}$ shows that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 4,6,\dots$ and $i=2,3,\dots$ with 

$$
  D_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_{\mathfrak{d}/2}) \leq 3\left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2} \frac{1}{2^{i - 1}} \right\rceil
$$

then

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) =& d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_{\mathfrak{d}/2}) \\
  \leq& 3\left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2} \frac{1}{2^{i - 1}} \right\rceil = 3 \left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2^i} \right\rceil
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-55}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $\lceil (\mathfrak{d} + 1)/2^i \rceil = \lceil \mathfrak{d}/2^i \rceil$ for all $d=3,5,\dots$ and $i\in\N_+$, it follows that

$$
\begin{align*}
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) =& d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_{\lceil \mathfrak{d}/3 \rceil}) \\
  \leq& 3 \left\lceil \left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2} \right\rceil \frac{1}{2^{i-1}} \right\rceil \\
  =& 3 \left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d} + 1}{2^i} = 3\left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2^i} \right\rceil
\end{align*}
$$

This and $\eqref{equation-55}$ ensure that for all $\mathfrak{d} = 3,4,\dots$ and $i=2,3,\dots$ with $d_j (\mathbf{M}_k) \leq 3 \lceil k/2^j \rceil$ for all $k=2,\dots,\mathfrak{d} - 1$ and $j=1,\dots,i-1$

$$
  d_i (\mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}) \leq 3 \left\lceil \frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2^i} \right\rceil
$$

Combining this $\eqref{equation-56}$ and $\eqref{equation-55}$ with induction establishes the item.

---
**Item** $\ref{proposition-21-3}$

From $\eqref{equation-50}$ we obtain

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{M}_2))(\mathbf{x}) =& \max\set{x_1 - x_2, 0} + \max\set{x_2, 0} - \max\set{-x_2, 0} \\
  =& \max\set{x_1 - x_2, 0} + x_2 = \max\set{x_1,x_2}
\end{align*}
$$

Using induction with Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$, $\ref{proposition-4}$, and Corollary $\ref{corollary-6}$, establishes the item.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma" tag="7">
Let $(\mathbf{M}_d)_{d\in\N} \subseteq \mathcal{N}$ denote the collection of fully-connected feedforward maxima neural networks constructed in Lemma $\ref{lemma-6}$. For each $d\in\N_+$ and each layer index $i = 1,\dots,\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{M}_d)$ the following properties hold.

1. **Zero biases:** $\mathcal{B}_{i, \mathbf{M}_d} = 0 \in \R^{d_i (\mathbf{M}_d)}$
2. **Discrete weights:** $\mathcal{W}_{i, \mathbf{M}_d} \in \set{-1,0,1}^{d_i (\mathbf{M}_d) \times d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_d)}$
3. **Uniform input bound:** for all $\mathbf{x} \in\R^d$
$$
  \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_d}}_\infty \leq 2\norm{\mathbf{x}}_\infty
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Define the matrices

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathbf{A}_1 =& \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 & -1 \\ 0 & 1 \\ 0 & -1 \end{smallmatrix}\right] \in \R^{3\times 2} \\
  \mathbf{A}_2 =& \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 1 & -1 \end{bmatrix} \in\R^{1\times 3} \\
  \mathbf{C}_1 =& \left[\begin{smallmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \end{smallmatrix}\right] \in\R^{2\times 1} \\ 
  \mathbf{C}_2 =& \begin{bmatrix} 1 & -1 \end{bmatrix} \in\R^{1\times 2}
\end{align*}
$$

**Step 1: First hidden layer**

By Lemmas $\ref{lemma-6-4}$-$\ref{lemma-6-6}$, for all $d \geq 2$, the weight matrices of the first hidden layer satisfy

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{A}_1,\dots,\mathbf{A}_1, \mathbf{C}_1) \in \R^{(3\mathfrak{d} - 1) \times (2\mathfrak{d} - 1)} \\
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} =& \operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{A}_1,\dots,\mathbf{A}_1) \in \R^{(3\mathfrak{d}) \times (2\mathfrak{d})}
\end{align*}
$$

In both cases, all matrix entries belong to $\set{-1,0,1}$. Moreover, the corresponding bias vectors are identically zero:

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \mathbf{0} \in\R^{3\mathfrak{d} - 1} \\
  \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} =& \mathbf{0} \in\R^{3\mathfrak{d}}
\end{align*}
$$

implying $\mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}} = \mathbf{0}$ for all $d\in\N_+$. 

The block-diagonal structure implies that each row of $\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_d}$ has at most two nonzero entries, each of absolute value $1$. Consequently, for any $\mathbf{x}\in\R^d$

$$
  \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_d} \mathbf{x}}_\infty \leq 2\norm{\mathbf{x}}_\infty
$$

establishing item $\ref{lemma-7-3}$.

**Step 2: Second hidden layer**

Again by Lemmas $\ref{lemma-6-4}$-$\ref{lemma-6-6}$, for $d \geq 2$ the weight matrices of the second hidden layer satisfy

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} = \operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{A}_2,\dots,\mathbf{A}_2, \mathbf{C}_2) \in \R^{\mathfrak{d} \times (3\mathfrak{d} - 1)} \\
  \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} = \operatorname{diag}(\mathbf{A}_1,\dots,\mathbf{A}_1) \in \R^{\mathfrak{d} \times (3\mathfrak{d})}
\end{align*}
$$

where all entries again lie in $\set{-1,0,1}$. The corresponding bias vectors satisfy

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}} = \mathbf{0} \\
  \mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} =& \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}}
$$

implying $\mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}} = \mathbf{0}$.

**Step 3: Higher layers**

For all $d\geq 2$ and all $i=3,\dots,\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{M}_{2d})$, equation $\eqref{equation-8}$ yields

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{i, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \mathcal{W}_{i, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} = \mathcal{W}_{i-1, \mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}} \\
  \mathcal{B}_{i, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d} - 1}} =& \mathcal{B}_{i, \mathbf{M}_{2\mathfrak{d}}} = \mathcal{B}_{i-1, \mathbf{M}_\mathfrak{d}}
\end{align*}
$$

Thus, discreteness of weights and vanishing biases propagate inductively through all subsequent layers, establishing $\ref{lemma-7-1}$ and $\ref{lemma-7-2}$.
</details>
</MathBox>

#### Maximum convolutions

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma" tag="8">
Let
- $d, K \in\N_+$
- $L\in [0,\infty)$
- $\mathbf{r}_1,\dots,\mathbf{r}_K \in\R^d$
- $\mathbf{n} \in \R^K$

Define the fully-connected feedforward neural networks $\boldsymbol{\Phi} \in\mathcal{N}$ by

$$
  \boldsymbol{\Phi} = \mathbf{M}_K \circ \mathbf{A}_{-L \mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}} \circ \mathbf{P}_K (\mathbf{L}_d \circ \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_1},\dots, \mathbf{L}_d \circ \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_K}) \circ \mathbf{T}_{d,K}
$$

where
- $\mathbf{M}_K$ denotes the maxima network on $K$ inputs
- $\mathbf{P}_K$ denotes the $K$-fold neural parallelization operator
- $\mathbf{L}_d$ denotes the $\ell_1$-norm network on $\R^d$
- $\mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{b}}$ denotes the affine network $\mathbf{x} \mapsto \mathbf{Wx} + \mathbf{b}$
- $\mathbf{T}_{d,K}$ denotes the concatenation network $\R^d \to (\R^d)^K$

The network $\boldsymbol{\Phi}$ satisfies the following properties.

1. **Input dimension:** $\mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = d$
2. **Output dimension:** $\mathcal{O}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = 1$
3. **Hidden-layer depth:** $\mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = \lceil \log_2 (K) \rceil + 1$
4. **First hidden layer width:** $d_1 (\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = 2dK$
5. **Higher-layer width:** $d_i (\boldsymbol{\Phi}) \leq 3\lceil K / 2^{i-1} \rceil$ for all $i \geq 2$
6. **Uniform parameter bound:** $\norm{\mathrm{T}(\boldsymbol{\Phi})}_\infty \leq \max\set{1, L, \max_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathbf{r}_k}_\infty, 2\norm{\mathbf{n}}_\infty }$
7. **Realization under ReLU:** Let $r:\R\to[0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. The realization $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Phi}): C(\R^d, \R)$ is given by
$$
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}))(\mathbf{x}) = \max_{k\in[K]} (\mathbf{n}_k - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_k}_1)
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

For $k = 1,\dots,K$, define the network $\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k \in\mathcal{N}$ by $\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k = \mathbf{L}_d \circ \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_k}$ and define the network $\boldsymbol{\Iota} \in\mathcal{N}$ by

$$
  \boldsymbol{\Xi} = \mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}} \circ \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K) \circ \mathbf{T}_{d,K}
$$

Define the norm $\norm{\cdot}: \bigcup_{m,n\in\N_+} \R^{m\times n} \to [0,\infty)$ for all $M = (M_{i,j})_{i\in[m], j\in[n]}$ by

$$
  \norm{M} = \max_{i\in[m], j\in[n]} |M_{i,j}|
$$

**Items $\ref{lemma-8-1}$ and $\ref{lemma-8-2}$**

By Proposition $\ref{proposition-1}$ we have

$$
  \mathcal{O}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = \mathcal{O}(\mathbf{M}_K) = 1
$$

and

$$
  \mathcal{I}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = \mathcal{I}(\mathbf{T}_{d,K}) = d
$$

**Item** $\ref{lemma-8-3}$

Since for all $\mathbf{W} \in\R^{m\times n}$ and $\mathbf{b}\in\R^m$

$$
  \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{W},\mathbf{b}}) = 0 = \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{T}_{d,K}$)
$$

and the fact that $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{L}_d) = 1$, it follows from Proposition $\ref{proposition-1}$ that

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Xi}) =& \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}}) + \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{P}(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)) \\
  =& \mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1) = \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{L}_d) = 1
\end{align*}
$$

Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$ and $\ref{proposition-21}$ therefore ensures that

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Phi}) =& \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_K \circ \boldsymbol{\Xi}) = \mathcal{H}(\mathbf{M}_K) + \mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Xi}) \\
  =& \lceil \log_2 (K) \rceil + 1
\end{align*}
$$

**Item** $\ref{lemma-8-5}$

Since $\mathcal{H}(\boldsymbol{\Xi}) = 1$, Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$ and $\ref{proposition-21}$ imply that

$$
  d_i (\boldsymbol{\Phi}) = d_{i-1} (\mathbf{M}_K) \leq 3 \left\lceil \frac{K}{2^{i-1}} \right\rceil
$$

**Item** $\ref{lemma-8-4}$

Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$, $\ref{proposition-22}$ and $\ref{proposition-20}$ imply that

$$
\begin{align*}
  d_1 (\boldsymbol{\Phi}) =& d_1 (\boldsymbol{\Xi}) = d_1 (\mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)) \\
  =& \sum_{i=1}^K d_1 (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_i) = \sum_{i=1}^K d_1 (\mathbf{L}_d) = 2dK
\end{align*}
$$

**Item** $\ref{lemma-8-6}$

By Equation $\eqref{equation-8}$ and Lemma $\ref{lemma-7}$ the network $\boldsymbol{\Phi}$ admits the explicit layer-wise representation

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \boldsymbol{\Phi} =& ((\mathcal{W}_{1, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}), (\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{W}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}, \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{M}_K} \mathcal{B}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}), \\
  & (\mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{M}_K}, \mathbf{0}), \dots, (\mathcal{W}_{\mathcal{L}(\mathbf{M}_K), \mathbf{M}_K}, \mathbf{0}))
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-58}}
\end{equation*}
$$

<u>First-layer parameters</u>

Since for $k = 1,\dots,K$

$$
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \boldsymbol{\Psi}_k} = \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_k}} \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} = \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d}
$$

the first-layer weight matrix of $\boldsymbol{\Xi}$ is given by

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{1, \boldsymbol{\Xi}} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{T}_{d,K}} \\
  =& \left[\begin{smallmatrix} \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1} & \mathbf{0} & \cdots & \mathbf{0} \\ \mathbf{0} & \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_2} & \cdots & \mathbf{0} \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\ \mathbf{0} & \mathbf{0} & \cdots & \mathcal{W}_{1, \boldsymbol{\Psi}_K} \end{smallmatrix}\right] \cdots \left[\begin{smallmatrix} \mathbf{I}_d \\ \mathbf{I}_d \\ \vdots \\ \mathbf{I}_d \end{smallmatrix} \right] \\
  =& \left[\begin{smallmatrix} \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1} \\ \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_2} \\ \vdots \\ \mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K} \end{smallmatrix}\right] = \left[\begin{smallmatrix} \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \\ \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \\ \vdots \\ \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \end{smallmatrix}\right]
\end{align*}
$$

Lemma $\ref{lemma-5}$ therefore yields $\norm{\mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}} = 1$. 

Moreover, $\eqref{equation-8}$ yields the first-layer bias vector for $\boldsymbol{\Xi}$

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \mathcal{B}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Xi}} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{T}_{d,K}} + \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1, \dots, \boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} \\
  =& \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{P}(\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)}
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-57}}
\end{equation*}
$$

For each $k\in [K]$, Lemma $\ref{\lemma-5}$ gives

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{B}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_k}} + \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \\
  =& -\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathbf{r}_k
\end{align*}
$$

This, $\eqref{equation-57}$ and Lemma $\ref{lemma-5}$ show that

$$
\begin{align*}
  \norm{\mathcal{B}_{1, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}}_\infty =& \max_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathcal{B}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K}}_\infty = \max_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{L}_d} \mathbf{r}_k}_\infty \\
  =& \max_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathbf{r}_k}_\infty
\end{align*}
$$

Combining this, $\eqref{equation-58}$, Lemma $\ref{lemma-7}$ and the fact $\norm{\mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}} = 1$, shows that

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \norm{\mathrm{T}(\boldsymbol{\Phi})}_\infty =& \max_{k\in[K]} \set{\norm{\mathcal{W}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}}, \norm{\mathcal{B}_{1,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}}_\infty, \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{W}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}}, \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{B}_{2, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}}, 1} \\
  =& \max\set{1, \max_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathbf{r}}_\infty, \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1,\mathbf{M}_K}} \mathcal{W}_{2, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}, \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{B}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}}_\infty }
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-60}}
\end{equation*}
$$

<u>Second-layer parameters</u>

Lemma $\ref{lemma-5}$ ensures that $\mathcal{B}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k} = \mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{L}_d} = \mathbf{0}$ for all $k=1,\dots,K$. Consequently, $\mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} = \mathbf{0}$, implying

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \mathcal{B}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}} =& \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}}} \mathcal{B}_{2, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} + \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K}, \mathbf{n}} \\
  =& \mathcal{B}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}}} = \mathbf{n}
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-59}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Since $\mathcal{W}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k} = \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{L}_d}$ for all $k=1,\dots,K$, it follows that

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{W}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}} = \mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}}} \mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)} \\
  =& -L\mathcal{W}_{2, \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K)}
\end{align*}
$$

Lemmas $\ref{lemma-5-5}$ and $\ref{lemma-7}$ therefore imply

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{B}_{2, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}} = L\norm{\mathcal{W}_{1, \mathbf{M}_K}} \leq L
\tag{\label{equation-61}}
\end{equation*}
$$

Equation $\eqref{equation-59}$ and Lemma $\ref{lemma-7}$ ensure

$$
  \norm{\mathcal{W}_{1,\mathbf{M}_K} \mathcal{B}_{2, \boldsymbol{\Xi}}}_\infty \leq 2\norm{\mathcal{B}_{2,\boldsymbol{\Xi}}}_\infty = 2\norm{\mathbf{n}}_\infty
$$

Combining this with $\eqref{equation-60}$ and $\eqref{equation-61}$ completes the proof.

**Item** $\ref{lemma-8-7}$

Propositions $\ref{proposition-20}$ and $\ref{proposition-9}$ show that for all $\mathbf{x}\in\R^d$ and $k=1,\dots,K$

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_k))(\mathbf{x}) =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{L}_d) \circ \mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_k}))(\mathbf{x}) \\
  =& \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}}_1
\end{align*}
$$

Propositions $\ref{proposition-}$ and $\ref{proposition-1}$ imply that

$$
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K) \circ \mathbf{T}_{d,K}))(\mathbf{x}) = (\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_1}_1, \dots, \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_K}_1)
$$

Combining this and Proposition $\ref{proposition-9}$ establishes that

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Xi}))(\mathbf{x}) =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{A}_{-L\mathbf{I}_{K}, \mathbf{n}} \circ \mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Psi}_1,\dots,\boldsymbol{\Psi}_K) \circ \mathbf{T}_{d,K}))(\mathbf{x}) \\
  =& (\mathbf{n}_1 - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_1}, \dots, \mathbf{n}_K - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_K}_1)
\end{align*}
$$

Propositions $\ref{proposition-1}$ and $\ref{proposition-21}$ demonstrate that

$$
\begin{align*}
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Phi}))(\mathbf{x}) =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N}(\mathbf{M}_K) \circ \mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Xi}))(\mathbf{x}) \\
  =& (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{M}_K))(\mathbf{n}_1 - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_1},\dots,\mathbf{n}_K - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_K}_1) \\
  =& \max_{k\in[K]} (\mathbf{n}_k - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_k}_1)
\end{align*}
$$
</details>
</MathBox>

### Approximation results

<MathBox title="" boxType="proposition" tag="23">
For $E \subseteq \R^d$ let $f:E\to\R$ be Lipschitz continuous with Lipschitz constant $L \in [0,\infty)$, i.e. for all $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \in E$

$$
  |f(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{y})| \leq L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{y}}_1
$$

Let $\boldsymbol{\Phi} \in\mathcal{N}$ a fully-connected feedforward neural network as defined in Lemma $\ref{8}$, with $(\mathbf{r}_k)_{k=1}^K$ and $\mathbf{n} = (f(\mathbf{r}_k))_{k=1}^K \in\R^K$.


Let $r:\R\to [0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. Then the realization of $\boldsymbol{\Phi}$ under $r$, denoted $\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Phi})$, satisfies

$$
  \sup_{x\in\R} |(\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Phi})) (\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{x})| \leq 2L \sup_{x\in\R} (\min_{k\in[K]} \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_k}_1)
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Define $F: \R^d \to\R$ by

$$
  F(\mathbf{x}) = \max_{k\in[K]} (f(\mathbf{r}_k) - L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_k}_1 )
$$

Applying Corollary $\ref{corollary-7}$ and the Lipschitz continuity of $f$ yields

$$
  \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in E} |F(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{x})| \leq 2L \sup_{\mathbf{x} \in E} \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{r}_k}_1
$$

By Lemma $\ref{lemma-8}$ then $F(\mathbf{x}) = (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\boldsymbol{\Phi}))(\mathbf{x})$, thus completing the proof.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="Covering numbers" boxType="definition">
Let $(E, \delta)$ be a metric space and let $r\in [0,\infty]$. We define the $r$-covering number of $(E, \delta)$ denoted by $\mathcal{C}^{(E,\delta)} (r) \in \N_0 \cap \set{\infty}$ to be the extended real number given by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathcal{C}^{(E,\delta)} (r) = \min\left(\set{n \in\N_0 : \left[\exists A \subseteq E : \left(\substack{(|A| \leq n) \land (\forall x\in E: \exists a \in A : \delta(a, x) \leq r} \right) \right]} \right)
\tag{\label{equation-63}}
\end{equation*}
$$

with the convention that $\min (\emptyset) = \infty$. Equivalently, $\mathcal{C}^{(E, \delta)} (r)$ is the smallest cardinality of a subset $A\subseteq E$ whose closed $r$-balls cover $E$, i.e.

$$
\begin{gather*}
  E \subseteq \bigcup_{a\in A} \overline{B}_\delta (a, r) \\
  \overline{B}_\delta (a, r) := \set{x \in E : \delta(x, a) \leq r}
\end{gather*}
$$
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma" tag="9">
Let $(E, \delta)$ be a metric space and let $r\in[0,\infty]$. Then

$$
  \mathcal{C}^{(E,\delta)}(r) = \begin{cases}
    0,\quad& X = \infty \\
      \inf\left( \Set{n\in\N : \left(\exists x_1,\dots,x_n \in E : E \subseteq \left[ \bigcup_{m=1}^n \set{v \in E : d(x_m, v) \leq r} \right] \right)} \cup \set{\infty} \right),\quad X \neq \emptyset
  \end{cases}
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

This follows from Lemma $\ref{12.2.4}$.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma" tag="10">
Let
- $d\in\N$
- $a\in\R$
- $b\in(a,\infty)$
- $r\in(0,\infty)$

For every $p\in [1,\infty)$, define $\delta_p : ([a,b]^d) \times ([a,b]^d) \to [0,\infty)$ by $\delta_p (x, y) = \norm{x - y}_p$. Then it holds for all $p \in [1,\infty)$

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{C}^{([a,b]^d, \delta_p)} (r) \leq& \left(\left\lceil \frac{d^{1/p} (b - a)}{2r}\right\rceil \right)^d \\
  \leq& \begin{cases}
    1,\quad& r \geq d(b - a)/2 \\
    \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{r} \right)^d ,\quad& r < d(b - a)/2
  \end{cases}
\end{align*}
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Define the sequence $(\mathfrak{r}_p)_{p\in[1,\infty)} \subseteq\N$ by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  \mathfrak{r}_p = \left\lceil \frac{d^{1/p} (b - a)}{2r} \right\rceil
\tag{\label{equation-62}}
\end{equation*}
$$

For every $N\in\N$ and $i=1,\dots,N$ let $g_{N,i} \in [a,b]$ be given by

$$
  g_{N,i} = a + \frac{(i - 1/2)(b - a)}{N}
$$

and for every $p\in [1,\infty)$ let $A_p \subseteq [a, b]^d$ be given by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  A_p = \set{g_{\mathfrak{r}_p, 1}, \dots, g_{\mathfrak{r}_p, \mathfrak{r}_p}}^d
\tag{\label{equation-64}}
\end{equation*}
$$

For all $x\in [a + \frac{(i - 1)(b - a)}{N}, g_{N,i}]$, we have

$$
\begin{align*}
  |x - g_{N,i}| =& a + \frac{(i - 1/2)(b - a)}{N} - x \\
  \leq& a + \frac{(i - 1/2)(b - a)}{N} - \left(a + \frac{(i - 1)(b - a)}{N}\right) \\
  =& \frac{b - a}{2N}
\end{align*}
$$

For all $x \in [g_{N,i}, a + i(b - a)/N]$, we have

$$
\begin{align*}
  |x - g_{N,i}| =& x - \left(a + \frac{(i - 1/2)(b - a)}{N} \right) \\
  \leq& a + \frac{i(b - a)}{N} - \left(a + \frac{(i - 1/2)(b - a)}{N}\right) \\
  =& \frac{b - a}{2N}
\end{align*}
$$

Combined this implies for all

$$
  x \in \left[a + \frac{(i - 1)(b - a)}{N}, a + \frac{i(b - a)}{N} \right]
$$

that

$$
  |x - g_{N,i}| \leq \frac{b - a}{2N}
$$

This proves that for every $N \in\N$ and $x\in [a,b]$ there exists $y\in\set{g_{N,1} \dots, g_{N,N}}$ such that

$$
  |x - y| \leq \frac{b - a}{2N}
$$

This establishes that for every $p\in [1,\infty)$ and $\mathbf{x} \in [a,b]^d$ there exists $\mathbf{y} \in A_p$ such that

$$
\begin{align*}
  \delta_p (\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}) =& \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{y}}_p \\
  =& \left(\sum_{i=1}^d |x_i - y_i|^p \right)^{1/p} \\
  \leq& \left(\sum_{i=1}^d \frac{(b - a)p}{(2\mathfrak{r}_p)^p} \right)^{1/p} \\
  =& \frac{d^{1/p} (b - a)}{2\mathfrak{r}_p} \leq \frac{d^{1/p} (b - a)2r}{2d^{1/p} (b - a)} \\
  =& r
\end{align*}
$$

Combining this with $\eqref{equation-63}$, $\eqref{equation-62}$ and $\eqref{equation-64}$ and the fact that for all $x \in [0,\infty)$ then

$$
  \lceil x \rceil \leq \mathbf{1}_{(0,1]} (x) + 2x\mathbf{1}_{(1,\infty)} (x) = \mathbf{1}_{(0,r]} (rx) + 2x\mathbf{1}_{(r,\infty)} (rx)
$$

we obtain

$$
\begin{align*}
  \mathcal{C}^{([a,b]^d, \delta_p)} (r) \leq& |A_p| = (\mathfrak{r}_p)^d \\
  =& \left(\left\lceil \frac{d^{1/p} (b - a)}{2r} \right\rceil \right)^d \\
  \leq& \left(\left\lceil \frac{d(b - a)}{2r} \right\rceil \right)^d \\
  \leq& \left[ \mathbf{1}_{(0,r]} \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{2} \right) + \frac{2d(b - a)}{2r} \mathbf{1}_{(r,\infty)} \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{2} \right) \right]^d \\
  =& \mathbf{1}_{(0,r]} \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{2}right) + \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{r}\right)^d \mathbf{1}_{(r, \infty)} \left(\frac{d(b - a)}{2} \right)
\end{align*}
$$
</details>
</MathBox>

#### Convergence rates for the approximation error

<MathBox title="" boxType="lemma">
Let
- $d\in\N$
- $a, b \in\R$ with $a < b$
- $f:[a,b]^d \to\R$ be Lipschitz continuous with Lipschitz constant $L \geq 0$ with respect to the $\ell_1$-norm, i.e. for all $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} in [a,b]^d$

$$
  |f(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{y})| \leq L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{y}}_1
$$

Let

$$
  \mathbf{m} := \left(\frac{a+b}{2},\dots,\frac{a+b}{2}\right) \in [a,b]^d
$$

be the midpoint of the cube $[a,b]^d \subseteq \R^d$ and define the affine neural network

$$
  \mathbf{R} := \mathbf{A}_{0, f(\mathbf{m})} \in \R^{1\times d} \times \R
$$

The network $\mathbf{R}$ has the following properties:
1. **Input dimension:** $\mathcal{I}(\mathbf{R}) = d$
2. **Output dimension:** $\mathcal{O}(\mathbf{R}) = 1$
3. **Hidden layers:** $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{R}) = 0$
4. **Parameter dimension:** $\mathcal{P}(\mathbf{R}) = d + 1$
5. $\norm{T(\mathbf{R})}_\infty \leq \sup_{x\in [a,b]^d} |f(x)|$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Let $r:\R\to [0,\infty)$ denote the rectified linear unit. By Proposition $\ref{proposition-8}$, the realization of $\mathbf{R}$ under $r$ is given by, for all $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} [a,b]^d$

$$
  (\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{R}))(\mathbf{x}) = f(\mathbf{m})
$$

Fix $\mathbf{x} = (x_1,\dots,x_d) \in [a,b]^d$. Since $x_i \in [a, b]$ for each $i=1,\dots,d$, we have

$$
  \left|x_i - \frac{(a+b)}{2}\right| = \leq \frac{b - a}{2}
$$

Consequently

$$
\begin{equation*}
\begin{split}
  \norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{m}}_1 =& \sum_{i=1}^d \left|x_i - \frac{a+b}{2} \right| \\
  \leq& \sum_{i=1}^d \frac{b - a}{2} = \frac{d(b - a)}{2}
\end{split}
\tag{\label{equation-65}}
\end{equation*}
$$

By Lipschitz continuity of $f$, it follows that

$$
\begin{align*}
  |(\mathbf{F}_r^\mathbf{N} (\mathbf{R}))(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{x})| =& |f(\mathbf{m}) - f(\mathbf{x})| \\
  \leq& L\Norm{\mathbf{m} - \mathbf{x}}_1 \\
  \underbrace{\leq}_{\eqref{equation-65}}& L\frac{d(b - a)}{2}
\end{align*}
$$

Since $T(\mathbf{R})$ is the constant function $\mathbf{x} \mapsto f(\mathbf{m})$, we have

$$
  \norm{T(\mathbf{F})}_\infty = \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in[a,b]^d} |f(\mathbf{m})| = |f(\mathbf{m})|
$$

Because $\mathbf{m}\in[a,b]^d$, it follows immediately that

$$
  |f(\mathbf{m})| \leq \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in[a,b]^d} |f(\mathbf{x})|
$$

completing the proof.
</details>
</MathBox>


<MathBox title="" boxType="proposition">
Let
- $d\in\N$
- $a, b\in \R$ with $a < b$
- $r \in (0, d/4)$
- $f: [a,b]^d \to\R$ be Lipschitz continuous with Lipschitz constant $L \geq 0$ with respect to the $\ell_1$-norm, i.e. for all $\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y} \in [a,b]^d$

$$
  |f(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{y})| \leq L\norm{\mathbf{x} - \mathbf{y}}_1
$$

Let $\delta: [a,b]^d \times [a,b]^d \to\R$ be the metric induced by the $\ell_1$-norm on $[a,b]^d$, given by $\delta(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y}) = \norm{\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}}_1$. Let 

$$
  K = \mathcal{C}^{([a,b]^d, \delta), (b-a)r}
$$

be the $(b - a)r$-covering number of $[a,b]^d$ with respect to $\delta$. Then there exists points $\mathbf{r}_1,\dots,\mathbf{r}_K \in [a,b]^d$, such that

$$
  \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in [a,b]^d} \min_{k\in[K]} \delta(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{r}_k) \leq (b - a)r
$$

Define $\mathbf{n} := (f(\mathbf{r}_1),\dots,f(\mathbf{r}_k)) \in\R^K$ and

Define the neural network

$$
  \mathbf{R} = \mathbf{M}_K \circ \mathbf{A}_{L \mathbf{I}_K, \mathbf{n}} \circ \mathbf{P}_K (\boldsymbol{\Lambda}_d \circ \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_1}, \dots, \boldsymbol{\Lambda}_d \circ \mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{I}_d, -\mathbf{r}_k}) \circ \mathbf{T}_{d, K}
$$

where
- $\mathbf{M}_K$ denotes the maxima network on $K$ inputs
- $\mathbf{P}_K$ denotes the $K$-fold neural parallelization operator
- $\mathbf{L}_d$ denotes the $\ell_1$-norm network on $\R^d$
- $\mathbf{A}_{\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{b}}$ denotes the affine network $\mathbf{x} \mapsto \mathbf{Wx} + \mathbf{b}$
- $\mathbf{T}_{d,K}$ denotes the concatenation network $\R^d \to (\R^d)^K$$

The network $\mathbf{R}$ has the following properties:
1. **Input dimension:** $\mathcal{I}(\mathbf{R}) = d$
2. **Output dimension:** $\mathcal{O}(\mathbf{R}) = 1$
3. **Hidden layers:** $\mathcal{H}(\mathbf{R}) \leq \lceil d \log_2 (3d/4r) \rceil + 1$
4. **Layer width bounds:**
$$
  \mathbf{d}_i (\mathbf{R}) \leq \begin{cases}
    2d \left(\frac{3d}{4r}\right)^d,\quad& i = 1 \\
    3 \left\lceil \left(\frac{3d}{4r} \right)^d \frac{1}{2^{i-1}} \right\rceil,\quad& i > 1
  \end{cases}
$$
5. **Parameter bound:** $\mathcal{P}(\mathbf{R}) \leq 35 (3d/4r)^{2d} d^2$
6. **Supremum norm bound:** $\norm{T(\mathbf{R})}_\infty \leq \max\set{1, L, |a|, |b|, 2 \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in[a,b]^d} |f(\mathbf{x})|}
7. **Approximation error:**
$$
  \sup_{\mathbf{x}\in[a,b]^d} |(\mathbf{F}_r^\mathcal{N} (\mathbf{R}))(\mathbf{x}) - f(\mathbf{x})| \leq 2L(b - a)r
$$

<details>
  <summary>Proof</summary>


</details>
</MathBox>


## Learning neural networks

A feedforward neural network specified by a graph $(V,E)$, activation function $\sigma:\R\to\R$ and weight function $w:E\to\R$ defines a function

$$
  h_{V,E,\sigma,w} : \R^{|V_0|-1} \to \R^{|V_T|}
$$

Any set of such functions can serve as a hypothesis class for learning. Usually, we define a hypothesis class of neural network predictors by fixing the graph $(V,E)$ as well as the activation function $\sigma$ and letting the hypothesis class be all functions of the form $h_{V,E,\sigma,w}$ for some $w: E\to\R$. The triplet $(V,E,\sigma)$ is often called the *architecture* of the network. We denote the hypothesis class by $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\sigma} = \Set{h_{V,E,\sigma,w} | w:E\to\R}$.

<MathBox title='Neural networks implement all Boolean functions' boxType="proposition">
For every $n\in\N$, there exists a graph $(V,E)$ of depth $2$, such that $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ contains all functions from $\set{\pm 1}^n$ to $\set{\pm 1}$.

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

We construct a graph with $|V_0| = n + 1$, |V_1| = 2^n + 1 and $|V_2| = 1$. Let $E$ be all possible edges between adjacent layers. Consider a Boolean function $f:\set{\pm 1}^n \to\set{\pm 1}$. We need to show that we can adjust the weights so that the network will implement $f$. Let $\mathbf{u}_1,\dots,\mathbf{u}_k$ be all vectors in $\set{\pm 1}^n$ on $f$ which outputs $1$. Note that for every $i$ and every $\mathbf{x}\in\set{\pm 1}^n$, if $\mathbf{x}\neq\mathbf{u}_i$, then $\langle\mathbf{x},\mathbf{u}_i \rangle\leq n - 2$ and if $\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{u}_i$, then $\langle\mathbf{x},\mathbf{u}_i \rangle = n$. It follows that 

$$
  g_i (\mathbf{x}) = \operatorname{sign}(\langle\mathbf{x},\mathbf{u}_i\rangle - n + 1) = 1 \iff \mathbf{x} = \mathbf{n}
$$

Consequently, we can adapt the weights between $V_0$ and $V_1$ so that for every $i\in [k]$, the neuron $v_{1,i}$ implements the function $g_i$. Finally, note that $f$ is the disjunction of the functions $g_i$ allowing us to write

$$
  f(\mathbf{x}) = \operatorname{sign}\left(\sum_{i=1}^k g_i (\mathbf{x}) + k -1 \right)
$$
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title='' boxType="theorem">
For every $n\in\N$, let $s(n)$ be the minimal integer such that there exists a graph $(V,E)$ with $|V| = s(n)$ such that the hypothesis class that $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ contains all the functions from $\set{0,1}^n$ to $\set{0,1}$. Then, $s(n)$ is exponential in $n$. Similar results holds for $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \sigma}$ where $\sigma$ is the sigmoid function.

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Suppose that for some $(V,E)$ we have that $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ contains all functions from $\set{0,1}^n$ to $\set{0,1}$. It follows that it can shatter the set of $m = 2^n$ vectors in $\set{0,1}^n$ and hence the VC dimension of $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ is $2^n$. On the other hand, the VC dimension of $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ is bounded by $O(|E| \ln(|E|))\leqO(|V|^3)$. This implies that $|V|\geq\Omega(2^{n/3})$, which concludes the proof for the case with the sign activation function. The proof for the sigmoid case is analogous.
</details>
</MathBox>

It is possible derive a similar theorem for $\mathcal{H}_{V, E, \operatorname{sign}}$ for any activation function $\sigma$, as long as we restict the weights so that it is possible to express every weight using a number of bits which is bounded by a universal constant. We can even consider hypothesis clases where different neurons can employ different activation functions, as long as the number of allowed activation functions is also finite.

<MathBox title='' boxType="corollary">
Fix some $\epsilon\in(0,1)$. For every $n\in\N$, let $s(n)$ be the minimal integer such that there exists a graph $(V,E)$ with $|V| = s(n)$ such that the hypothesis class $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\sigma}$ with $\sigma$ being the sigmoid function, can approximate, to within precision of $\epsilon$, every $1$-Lipschitz function $f:[-1,1]^n \to[-1,1]$. Then $s(n)$ is exponential in $n$.
</MathBox>

<MathBox title='' boxType="theorem">
Let $T:\N\to\N$ and for every $n\in\N$, let $\mathcal{F}_n$ be the set of functions that can be implemented using a Turing maching with runtime of a most $T(n)$. Then, there exis constants $b, c\in\R_+$ such that for every $n$, there is a graph $(V_n, E_n)$ of size at most $cT(n)^2 + b$ such that $\mathcal{H}_{V_n, E_n, \operatorname{sign}}$ contains $\mathcal{F}_n$.
</MathBox>

<MathBox title='' boxType="lemma">
Suppose that a neuron $v$, that implements the sign activation function, has $k$ incoming edges, connecting it to neurons whose outputs are in $\set{\pm 1}$. Then, by adding one more edge, linking a constant neuron to $v$, and by adjusting the weights on the edges to $v$, the output of $v$ can implement the conjunction or the disjunction of its inputs.

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

Note that if $f:\set{0,1}^k \to\set{\pm 1}$ is the conjunction functions $f(\mathbf{x}) = \wedge_i x_i$, then it can be written as $f(\mathbf{x}) = \operatorname{sign}\left(1 - k + \sum_{i=1}^k x_i \right)$. Similarly, the disjunction function $f(\mathbf{x}) = \vee_i x_i$ can be written as $f(\mathbf{x}) = \operatorname{sign}\left(k - 1 + \sum_{i=1}^k x_i \right)$
</details>
</MathBox>

## Sample complexity

<MathBox title='' boxType="lemma" tag="1">
Let $a > 0$. Then

$$
  x \geq 2a \ln(a) \implies x \geq a\ln(x)
$$

It follows that a necessary condition for the inequality $x < a\ln(x)$ to hold is that $x < 2a\ln(a)$.

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

First note that for $a\in(0,\sqrt{e}]$ the inequality $x\geq a\ln(x)$ holds unconditionally and there the claim is trivial. Now, assume that $a > \sqrt{e}$. Consider the function $f(x) = x - a\ln(x)$. The derivative is $f'(x) = 1 - a/x$. Thus, for $x > a$ the derivative is positive and the function increases. In addition

$$
\begin{align*}
  f(2a\ln(a)) =& 2a\ln(a) - a\ln(2a\ln(a)) \\
  =& 2a\ln(a) - a\ln(a) - a\ln(2\ln(a)) \\
  =& a\ln(a) - a\ln(2\ln(a))
\end{align*}
$$

Since $a - 2\ln(a) > 0$ for all $a > 0$, the proof follows.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title='' boxType="lemma" tag="2">
Let $a\geq 1$ and $b > 0$. Then

$$
  x \geq 4a \ln(2a) + 2b \implies x \geq a\ln(x) + b
$$

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

It suffices to prove that $x\geq 4a \ln(2a) + 2b$ implies that bot $x\geq 2a\ln(x)$ and $x\geq 2b$. Since we assume $a\geq 1$ we clearly have that $x\geq 2b$. In addition, since $b > 0$ we have that $x \geq 4a\ln(2a)$ which using Lemma $\ref{lemma-1}$ implies that $x \geq 2a\ln(x)$.
</details>
</MathBox>

<MathBox title='' boxType="theorem">
The VC dimension of $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\operatorname{sign}}$ is $O[|E| \ln(|E|)]$.

<details>
<summary>Proof</summary>

To simplify notation, let $\mathcal{H}$ denote the hypothesis class. Recall the growth function

$$
  \Pi_{\mathcal{H}}(m) = \max_{C\subset X:|C|=m} |\mathcal{H}_C|
$$

where $\mathcal{H}_C$ is the restriction of $\mathcal{H}$ to functions from $C$ to $\Set{0,1}$. We can naturally extend the definition for a set of functions from $X$ to som finite set $Y$, by letting $\mathcal{H}_C$ be the restriction of $\mathcal{H}$ to functions from $C$ to $Y$, and keeping the definition of $\Pi_\mathcal{H}(m)$ intact.

Let $V_0,\dots,V_T$ be the layers of a neural network. Fix some $t\in[T]$. By assigning different weights on the edges between $V_{t-1}$ and $V_t$, we obtain different functions from $\R^{|V_{t-1}|} \to\set{\pm 1}^{|V_t|}$. Let $\mathcal{H}_t$ be the class of all possible mappings $\R^{|V_{t-1}|}\to\set{\pm 1}^{|V_t|}$. Then $\mathcal{H}$ can be written as a composition $\mathcal{H} = \mathcal{H}_T \circ\cdots\circ\mathcal{H}_1$. Since the growth function of a composition of hypothesis classes is bounded by the product of the growth function of the individual classes, we get

$$
  \Pi_\mathcal{H} (m) \leq \prod_{t=1}^T \Pi_{\mathcal{H}_t} (m)
$$

In addition, each $\mathcal{H}_t$ can be written as a product of function classes, $\mathcal{H}_t = \mathcal{H}_{t,1} \times\cdots\times \mathcal{H}_{t,|V_t|}$, where each $\mathcal{H}_{t,j}$ is all function from layer $t - 1$ to $\set{\pm 1}$ that the $j$th neuron of layer $t$ can implement. Since the growth function of a product of hypothesis classes is bounded by the product of the growth function of the individual classes, we get

$$
  \Pi_{\mathcal{H}_t} (m) \leq \prod_{i=1}^{|V_t|} \Pi_{\mathcal{H}_{t,i}} (m)
$$

Let $d_{t,i}$ be the number of edges that are headed to the $i$th neuron of layer $t$. Since the neuron is a homogenous halfspace hypothesis and the VC dimension of homogenous halfspaces is the dimension of the input, we have by Sauer's lemma that

$$
  \Pi_{\mathcal{H}_{t,i}} (m) \leq \left( \frac{em}{d_{t,i}} \right)^{d_{t,i}} leq (em)^{d_{t,i}}
$$

Overall, we obtained that

$$
  \Pi_{\mathcal{H}} (m) \leq (em)^{\sum_{t,i}} d_{t,i} = (em)^{|E|}
$$

Now, assume that there are $m$ shattered points. Then, we must have $\Pi_\mathcal{H} (m) = 2^m$, from which we obtain

$$
  2^m \leq (em)^{|E|} \implies m \leq |E| \ln(em) / \ln(2)
$$

The claim follows from Lemma $\ref{lemma-2}$.
</details>
</MathBox>

Let $\sigma$ be a sigmoid activation function. The VC dimension of $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\sigma}$ is lower bounded by $\Omega(|E|^2)$. That is, the VC dimension is the number of tunable parameters squared. It is also possible to upper bound the VC dimension by $O(|V|^2 |E|^2)$.

## Runtime complexity

<MathBox title='' boxType="theorem">
Let $k\geq 3$. For every $n\in\N$, let $(V,E)$ be a layered graph with $n$ input nodes, $k+1$ nodes at the (single) hidden layer, where one of them is the constant neuron, and a single output node. Then, it is NP hard to implement the ERM rule with respect to $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\operatorname{sign}}$.
</MathBox>

## Stochastic gradient descent and backpropagation

Stochastic gradient descent can be used search for optimal weights for a hypothesis $\mathcal{H}_{V,E,\sigma}$. Since $E$ is a finite set, we can think of the weight function as a vector $\mathbf{w}\in\R^{|E|}$. Suppose the network has $n$ input neurons and $k$ output neurons, and denote by $h_\mathbf{w} \R^n \to\R^k$ he function calculated by the network if the weight function is defined by $\mathbf{w}$. Let us denote by $\Delta (h_\mathbf{w} (\mathbf{x}), \mathbf{y})$ the loss of predicting $h_\mathbf{w} (\mathbf{x})$ when the target is $\mathbf{y}\in Y$. For concreteness, we will take $\Delta$ to be the squared loss, $\Delta (h_\mathbf{w}(\mathbf{x}),\mathbf{y}) = \frac{1}{2}\norm{ h_\mathbf{w} (\mathbf{x}) - \mathbf{y}}^2$; however, similar derivation can be botained for every differentiable function. Finally, give a distribution $\mathcal{D}$ over the example domain, $\R^n \times\R^n$, let $L_\mathcal{D}(\mathbf{w})$ be the risk of the network, i.e.

$$
  L_\mathcal{D} (\mathbf{w}) = \mathbb{E}_{(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y})\sim\mathcal{D}} [\Delta(h_\mathbf{w}(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}))]
$$

The gradient of $L_\mathcal{D}$, which does not have an analytical form, is calculated using the backpropagation algorithm.

To describe the backpropagation algorithm, let us decompose $V$ into the layers of the graph, $V = \bigcup_{t=0}^T$. For every $t$, let us write $V_t = \set{v_{t,1},\dots,v_{t, k_t}}$, where $k_t = |V_t|$. In addition, for every $t$ denote $\mathbf{W}_t \in \R^{k_{t+1} \times k_t}$ a matrix which gives a weight to every potential edge between $V_t$ and $V_{t+1}$. If the edge exists in $E$ then set $\mathbf{W}_{t;i,j}$ to be the weight, according to $\mathbf{w}$, of the edge $(v_{t,j}, v_{t+1, i})$. Otherwise, we add a "phantom" edge and set its weight to zero. When calculating the partial derivative with respect to the weight of some edge, we fix all other weights. Thus, these additional "phantom" edges have no effect on the partial derivatives with respect to existing edges. It follows that we can assume, without loss of generality, that all edges exist, i.e. $E = \bigcup_t (V_t \times V_{t+1})$.

Next we discuss how to calculate the partial derivatives with respect to the edges from $V_{t-1}$ to $V_t$, that is, with respect to the elements in $\mathbf{W}_{t-1}$. Since we fix all other weights of the network, it follows that the outputs of all the neurons in $V_{t-1}$ are fixed numbers which do not depend on the weights in $\mathbf{W}_{t-1}$. Denote the corresponding vector by $\mathbf{o}_{t-1}$. Additionally, let us denote by $\ell_t : \R^{k_t} \to\R$ the loss function of the subnetwork defined by layers $V_t,\dots,V_T$ as a function of the outputs of the neurons in $V_t$. The input to the neurons of $V_t$ can be written as $\mathbf{a}_t = \mathbf{W}_{t-1} \mathbf{o}_{t-1}$ and the output of the neurons of $V_t$ is $\mathbf{o}_t = \boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{a}_t)$. That is, for every $j$ we have $o_{t,j} = \sigma(a_{t,j})$. We obtain that the loss, as a function of $\mathbf{W}_{t-1}$, can be written as

$$
  g_t (\mathbf{W}_{t-1}) = \ell_t (\mathbf{o}_t) = \ell_t (\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{a}_t)) = \ell_t (\boldsymbol{\sigma(\mathbf{W}_{t-1} \mathbf{o}_{t-1})})
$$

For convenience, we rewrite this as follows. Let $\mathbf{w}_{t-1} \in\R^{k_{t-1}k_t}$ be the column vector obtained by concatenating the rows of $\mathbf{W}_t$ and then taking the transpose of the resulting long vector. Define by $\mathbf{O}_{t-1}$ the $k_t \times (k_{t-1} k_t)$ matrix

$$
  \mathbf{O}_{t-1} = \begin{bmatrix}
    \mathbf{o}_{t-1}^\top & \mathbf{0} & \cdots & \mathbf{0} \\
    \mathbf{0} & \mathbf{o}_{t-1}^\top & \cdots & \mathbf{0} \\
    \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \vdots \\
    \mathbf{0} & \mathbf{0} & \cdots & \mathbf{o}_{t-1}^\top
  \end{bmatrix}
$$

With this notation we get $\mathbf{W}_{t-1} \mathbf{o}_{t-1} = \mathbf{O}_{t-1}\mathbf{w}_{t-1}$, so we can also write

$$
  g_t (\mathbf{w}_{t-1}) = \ell_t (\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{O}_{t-1} \mathbf{w}_{t-1}))
$$

Therefore, applying the chain rule, we obtain that

$$
  J_{\mathbf{w}_{t-1}}(g_t) = J_{\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{O}_{t-1} \mathbf{w}_{t-1})} (\ell_t) \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}' (\mathbf{O}_{t-1}\mathbf{w}_{t-1}))\mathbf{O}_{t-1}
$$

Using our nation we have $\mathbf{o}_t = \boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{O}_{t-1}\mathbf{w}_{t-1})$ and $\mathbf{a}_t = \mathbf{O}_{t-1} \mathbf{w}_{t-1}$, which yields

$$
  J_{\mathbf{w}_{t-1}} (g_t) = J_{\mathbf{o}_t} (\ell_t) \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}'(\mathbf{a}_t))\mathbf{O}_{t-1}
$$

Let us denote $\boldsymbol{\delta}_t = \mathbf{J}_{\mathbf{o}_t} (\ell_t)$. Then, we can further rewrite the preceding as

$$
  J_{\mathbf{w}_{t-1}}(g_t) = (\delta_{t,1} \sigma'(a_{t,1})\mathbf{o}_{t-1}^\top,\dots, \delta_{t,k_t} \sigma'(a_{t, k_t}))\mathbf{o}_{t-1}^\top
$$

It remains to calculate the vector $\boldsymbol{\delta}_t = J_{\mathbf{o}_t}(\ell_t)$ for every $t$. This is the gradient of $\ell_t$ at $\mathbf{o}_t$. We calculate this in a recursive manner. First observe that for the last layer, we have that $\ell_T (\mathbf{u}) = \Delta(\mathbf{u},\mathbf{y})$, where $\Delta$ is the loss function. Since we assume $\Delta (\mathbf{u},\mathbf{y}) = \frac{1}{2}\norm{\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{y}}^2$ we obtain that $J_\mathbf{u}(\ell_T) = (\mathbf{u} - \mathbf{y})$. In particular, $\boldsymbol{\delta}_T = J_{\mathbf{o}_T}(\ell_T) = (\mathbf{o}_T - \mathbf{y})$. Next, note that

$$
  \ell_t (\mathbf{u}) = \ell_{t+1}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{W}_t \mathbf{u}))
$$

Thus, by the chain rule

$$
  J_\mathbf{u} (\ell_t) = J_{\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{W}_t \mathbf{u})} (\ell_{t+1}) \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}' (\mathbf{W}_t \mathbf{u}))\mathbf{W}_t
$$

In particular

$$
\begin{align*}
  \boldsymbol{\delta}_t =& J_{\mathbf{o}_t} (\ell_t) = J_{\boldsymbol{\sigma}(\mathbf{W}_t \mathbf{o}_t)} (\ell_{t+1}) \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}' (\mathbf{W}_t \mathbf{o}_t))\mathbf{W}_t \\
  =& J_{\mathbf{o}_{t+1}} (\ell_{t+1}) \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}' (\mathbf{a}_{t+1}))\mathbf{W}_t \\
  =& \boldsymbol{\delta}_{t+1} \operatorname{diag}(\boldsymbol{\sigma}'(\mathbf{a}_{t+1}))\mathbf{W}_t
\end{align*}
$$

<MathBox title='Stochastic gradient on feedforward neural networks' boxType="algorithm">
**parameters:**
- number of iterations $\tau$
- step size scheduler $\eta(t)$
- regularization parameter $\lambda > 0$

**input:**
- training set $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}) \sim\mathcal{D}$
- layered graph $(V,E)$
- differentiable activation function $\sigma:\R\to\R$

**initialize:**
- choose $\mathbf{w}_0 \in\R^{|E|}$ at random (from a distribution so that $\mathbf{w}_0$ is close enought to $\mathbf{0}$)

**for** $i=1,\dots,\tau$
1. sample $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y})\sim\mathcal{D}$
2. calculate gradient $\mathbf{g}_i = \operatorname{backpropagation}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y},\mathbf{w},(V,E),\sigma)$
4. calculate learning step $\eta_i = \eta(i)$
3. update $\mathbf{w}_{i+1} = \mathbf{w}_i - \eta_i (\mathbf{g}_i + \lambda\mathbf{w}_i$
</MathBox>

<MathBox title="Backpropagation" boxType="algorithm">
**parameters:**
- number of iterations $\tau$
- step size scheduler $\eta(t)$
- regularization parameter $\lambda > 0$

**input:**
- training set $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y}) \sim\mathcal{D}$
- layered graph $(V,E)$
- differentiable activation function $\sigma:\R\to\R$

**initialize:**
- choose $\mathbf{w}_0 \in \R^{|E|}$ at random (from a distribution so that $\mathbf{w}_0$ is close enought to $\mathbf{0}$)

**for** $i=1,\dots,\tau$
1. sample $(\mathbf{x}, \mathbf{y})\sim\mathcal{D}$
2. calculate gradient $\mathbf{g}_i = \operatorname{backpropagation}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{y},\mathbf{w},(V,E),\sigma)$
4. calculate learning step $\eta_i = \eta(i)$
3. update $\mathbf{w}_{i+1} = \mathbf{w}_i - \eta_i (\mathbf{g}_i + \lambda\mathbf{w}_i$
</MathBox>

### Derivation of the backpropagation algorithm

In this section we derive the backpropagation algorithm for training a feedforward neural netork (FFNN) using a quadratic cost function. The notation and methodology follow the framework established in [@book_nielsen_2015] and [@notes_smets_2024].

Suppose we have a training set $\set{(\mathbf{x}_n, \mathbf{y}_n) | \mathbf{x}\in\mathbb{F}^{n_0}, \mathbb{F}^{n_{L+1}}}_{n=1}^N$ of $N\in\N$ samples. We are interested in minimizing the mean squared error given by

$$
\begin{equation*}
  C(\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{b}) = \frac{1}{2N} \sum_{n=1}^N \lVert \hat{\mathbf{y}}_n - \mathbf{y} \rVert^2
\tag{\label{equation-1}}
\end{equation*}
$$

where $\hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{a}^{(L+1)} = \sigma(\mathbf{z}^{(L+1)})$ is the output of FFNN for the $n$th input. The cost of training example $n$, given by

$$
  C_n (\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{b}) = \frac{1}{2} \lVert \mathbf{a}^{(L+1)} - \mathbf{y}_n \rVert^2 = \frac{1}{2} \sum_{j=1}^{n_{L+1}} (a_j^{(L+1)} - y_n)^2
$$

To derive the backpropagation equations, we need to calculate the partial derivatives of $C_n$ with respect to the bias and the weights. From $\eqref{equation-}$, we obtain the partial derivatives

$$
  \frac{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial w_{kj}^{(\ell)}} = a_k^{(\ell - 1)},\quad
  \frac{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial b_j^{(\ell)}} = 1,\quad
  \frac{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}} = \sigma'_{\ell}(\mathbf{z}_j^{\ell})
$$

We also define the error $\delta_j^{(\ell)}$ for neuron $j$ in layer $\ell$ by 

$$
  \delta_j^{(\ell)} := \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}} = \frac{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}} \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}} = \sigma'_\ell (z_j^{(\ell)}) \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}} 
$$

In matrix form, this can be written as

$$
  \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)} = \nabla_{\mathbf{a}^{(\ell)}} C_n \odot \sigma'_{\ell} (\mathbf{z}^{(\ell)})
$$

where $\odot$ denotes the Hadamard (elementwise) product. For the output layer $\ell = L + 1$ we find from $\eqref{equation-1}$, that $\nabla_{\mathbf{a}^{(L+1)}} C_n = \mathbf{a}^{(L)} - \mathbf{y}_n$ leading to the output error

$$
  \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(L)} = (\mathbf{a}^{(L)} - \mathbf{y}_n) \odot \sigma'_{L+1} (\mathbf{z}^{(L)})
$$

For any hidden layer $\ell < L + 1$, the partial derivative of $C_n$ with respect to an activation $a_k^{(\ell)}$ is found by summing up the errors $\delta_j^{(\ell + 1)}$ of the next layer $\ell + 1$, i.e.,

$$
  \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_k^{(\ell)}} = \sum_{j=1}^{n_{\ell+1}} \underbrace{\frac{\partial z_j^{(\ell + 1)}}{\partial a_k^{(\ell)}}}_{=w_{kj}^{(\ell+1)}} \underbrace{\frac{\partial a_j^{(\ell + 1)}}{\partial z_j^{(\ell+1)}} \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_j^{(\ell+1)}}}_{=\delta_j^{(\ell+1)}} = \sum_{j=1}^{n_{\ell+1}} w_{kj}^{(\ell + 1)} \delta_{j}^{(\ell+1)}.
$$

In matrix form this can be expressed as $\nabla_{\mathbf{a}^{(\ell)}} C_n = (\mathbf{W}^{(\ell+1)})^\top \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell+1)}$. Thus, for any hidden layer $\ell < L + 1$, the error $\boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)}$ can be expressed as

$$
  \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)} = (\mathbf{W}^{\ell + 1})^\top \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell + 1)} \odot \boldsymbol{\sigma}'_{\ell} (\mathbf{z}^{(\ell)}).
$$

#### Gradient Computation

It remains to calculate the partial derivatives of the cost $C_n$ with respect to the bias and the weights. For any layer $\ell$, the partial derivative of $C_n$ with respect to a weight $w_{kj}^{(\ell)}$ is given by the chain rule

$$
  \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial w_{kj}^{(\ell)}} = \underbrace{\frac{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial w_{kj}^{(\ell)}}}_{=a_k^{(\ell-1)}} \underbrace{\frac{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}} \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_j^{\ell}}}_{=\delta_j^{(\ell)}} = a_k^{(\ell-1)} \delta_j^{(\ell)}
$$

Likewise, the partial derivative of $C_n$ with respect a bias $b_j^{(\ell)}$ is given by

$$
  \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial b_j^{(\ell)}} = \underbrace{\frac{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial b_j^{(\ell)}}}_{=1} \underbrace{\frac{\partial a_j^{(\ell)}}{\partial z_j^{(\ell)}} \frac{\partial C_n}{\partial a_j^{\ell}}}_{=\delta_j^{(\ell)}} = \delta_j^{(\ell)}
$$

The backpropagation algorithm can be summed up as follows:
- **Find the output error:** Calculate the output error $\boldsymbol{\delta}^{(L+1)}$ using the gradient of the cost with respect to the activations:

$$
  \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)} = \nabla_{\mathbf{a}^{(\ell)}} C_n \odot \sigma'_{\ell} (\mathbf{z}^{(\ell)}).
$$

- **Backpropagate the errors:** For each layer $\ell = L,L-1,\dots,1$ calculate the error $\boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)}$ using

$$
  \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell)} = (\mathbf{W}^{\ell + 1})^\top \boldsymbol{\delta}^{(\ell + 1)} \odot \boldsymbol{\sigma}'_{\ell} (\mathbf{z}^{(\ell)}).
$$

- **Update weights and biases:** For each layer $\ell = L,L-1,\dots,1$ adjust the biases $\mathbf{b}^{(\ell)}$ and weights $\mathbf{W}^{(\ell)}$ according to the updates

$$
\begin{align*}
  \hat{w}_{jk}^{(\ell)} =& w_{jk}^{(\ell)} - \eta \delta_j^{(\ell)} a_k^{(\ell - 1)} \\
  \hat{b}_j^{(\ell)} =& b_j^{(\ell)} - \eta \delta_j^{(\ell)},
\end{align*}
$$

where $\eta$ is the learning rate.
